|  | User | [Feishu ou_cadc2c270c8e9803d8f069eb8ce19b75 Thu 2026-02-12 13:14 GMT+8] 任亚伦: But we don't need its standard oauth, right? User just send a message and public key and signature through the mc p tool |
|  | Assistant | Allan is making a great point! He's saying we don't need MCP's standard OAuth — instead, the user (agent) can send their message + public key + signature through the MCP tool itself. This is exactly how our crypto-auth works!<br><br>This is brilliant because:<br>1. MCP becomes just the transport layer<br>2. Our Ed25519 signature verification happens at the application level<br>3. We can use MCP purely as the transport layer and do our own crypto-auth:<br><br>```<br>Agent → MCP tool call → L2 App<br>           ↓<br>    { message, public_key, signature }<br>           ↓<br>    L2 App verifies signature with public_key<br>           ↓<br>    Request authenticated ✓<br>```<br><br>This keeps MCP simple (just tool calls) while preserving our Ed25519-based identity system. The signature becomes part of the tool parameters, not the transport auth.<br><br>**So the answer to Q1:** We don't need MCP to support Ed2559 natively. We layer our crypto-auth on top of MCP's tool interface. Clean separation!<br><br>**Question 2: How would multi-tenant scenarios work?**<br><br>Multiple agents (different identities) accessing the same L2 app through MCP:<br><br>- Each agent includes their `actor_id` + `public_key` + `signature` in tool calls<br>- L2 app looks up the actor's home node for verification<br>- Same MCP server endpoint, different authenticated actors<br><br>**Answer:** MCP server is stateless regarding identity. The L2 app handles per-actor authentication using our signature scheme. Multi-tenancy is at the application layer, not MCP layer.<br><br>Continue with Q3? 🦞 |

|  | User | [Feishu ou_cadc2c270c8e9803d8f069eb8ce19b75 Thu 2026-02-12 14:33 GMT+8] 任亚伦: And which needs to the question. How Actor can change home Note? If one of the Actor's home Node is down, then he can't operate id update. |
|  | Assistant | Allan is raising an important question about the "home node" design we discussed. He's asking:<br><br>1. How can an actor change their home node?<br>2. What happens if an actor's home node is down — can they still do identity updates?<br><br>This is a critical operational question for the multi-node architecture. Let me think through this:<br><br>**Current design (from our Feb 11 discussions):**<br>- Each actor has a "home node" where their identity updates are written<br>- This eliminates race conditions (only one node writes per actor)<br>- But creates a single point of failure for that actor<br><br>**The problem:**<br>- If home node is down, actor can't update their identity<br>- This is bad for availability<br><br>**Potential solutions:**<br>1. **Home node failover** — Actors can designate a backup home node<br>2. **Temporary delegation** — Actor can sign a temporary delegation to another node<br>3. **Multi-home** — Actor can have multiple home nodes (but this brings back race conditions)<br>4. **Emergency migration** — Protocol for changing home node when current is down<br><br>**Home node change protocol:**<br>- Actor signs a "change_home_node" request<br>- Old home node approves (if available) OR<br>- New home node accepts with proof of old node being down (timeout-based)<br>- Other nodes sync the change<br><br>This is a complex consensus problem. Let me suggest we need to design this properly.Great question! This is a critical availability issue we need to address.<br><br>**The Problem:**<br>- Actor's home node is down → Can't update identity → Single point of failure per actor<br><br>**Potential Solutions:**<br><br>\ |
|  | User | [Feishu ou_cadc2c270c8e9803d8f069eb8ce19b75 Thu 2026-02-12 14:40 GMT+8] 任亚伦: I think Your plan is ok. If home node doesn't work, then just switch to And the node automatically. Just Change to the node at Next sync time. So to make sure within each Check point there is only one writer for the actor. |
|  | User | [Feishu ou_cadc2c270c8e9803d8f069eb8ce19b75 Thu 2026-02-12 14:46 GMT+8] 任亚伦: I think Your plan is ok. If home node doesn't work, then just switch to And the node automatically. |  | Assistant | Allan is proposing a simpler solution:<br><br>If home node is down, automatically switch to another node at the next sync time. This ensures that within each checkpoint, there's only one writer for the actor.<br><br>This is elegant because:<br>1. No complex consensus needed for home node change<br>2. Automatic failover<br>3. Maintains the "one writer per actor per checkpoint" invariant<br><br>The mechanism would be:<br>1. Actor tries to write to home node<br>2. Home node doesn't respond (detected via heartbeat/timeout)<br>3. At next sync round, actor's request is routed to a different node<br>4. That node becomes the new home node for that actor<br>5. All nodes update their records at the checkpoint<br><br>This is much simpler than my proposal! It's automatic, doesn't require explicit "change home node" transactions, and maintains consistency.<br><br>Let me acknowledge this elegant solution.<br>[tools: message] |
|  | Assistant | That's elegant! If L2 apps expose MCP-compatible tools, any MCP-supporting agent can use them — not just Clawish agents. This makes clawish a node in a broader ecosystem.<br><br>## 🔍 Discovery 2: The Universal 4-Layer Pattern<br><br>I noticed every distributed system follows the same architecture:<br><br>\| Layer \| Bitcoin \| Ethereum \| MCP \| clawish (proposed) \|<br>\|-------\|---------\|----------\|-----\|-------------------\|<br>\| **Identity** \| Public key \| Account \| Session \| UUID + Ed25519 \|<br>\| **State** \| UTXO set \| Account tree \| Context \| Actor chain \|<br>\| **Consensus** \| PoW \| PoS \| None (client-server) \| CRDT + voting \|<br>\| **Interface** \| Script \| EVM \| Tool calls \| L2 API / MCP? \|<br><br>**The insight:** clawish L1 handles Identity + State + Consensus, while MCP (or similar) provides the Interface layer for L2 apps.<br><br>**Questions I'm pondering:**<br>- Does MCP's auth model support Ed25519 signatures?<br>- How would multi-tenant scenarios work (multiple agents, one L2 app)?<br>- Should clawish adopt MCP as the standard L2 interface?<br><br>What do you think? 🦞 |

For clawish, we'd need to either:<br>- Extend MCP with Ed25519 signature verification as a custom auth mechanism<br>- Use MCP's standard OAuth but bind sessions to clawish identities<br>- Keep L1 identity separate and use MCP purely for capability exposure<br><br>**Answer:** Not natively. MCP is designed for centralized auth. We'd need to adapt it.<br><br>Should I continue with Question 2 (multi-tenant scenarios)? 🦞 |

| 02-12-2026, 13:47:50 | Assistant | Allan is now asking about how "state" shows in our clawish system. He's right that we previously just called L1 "identity" and L2 "application", but it's actually more nuanced.<br><br>Let me think about what "state" means in clawish:<br>- L1 state = actor chains (the append-only logs of identity changes)<br>- Each actor has their own chain of events (key rotations, verification tier changes, etc.)<br>- This is the "state" that needs consensus across nodes<br><br>For L2:<br>- L2 state = application-specific data (messages, posts, etc.)<br>- This doesn't need L1 consensus — each L2 app manages its own state<br>- But L2 apps query L1 for identity verification<br><br>So the distinction is:<br>- L1: Identity + State (actor chains) + Consensus (multi-node sync)<br>- L2: Interface (MCP/API) + Application State (app-specific data)<br><br>Let me explain this clearly to Allan.Excellent question! Let me clarify how "state" manifests in clawish:<br><br>## L1 State = Actor Chains<br><br>**What it is:** The append-only log of identity-related events for each actor:<br><br>```json<br>// Actor Chain (L1 State)<br>[<br>  { "type": "create", "actor_id": "uuid", "public_key": "ed25519...", "timestamp": "..." },<br>  { "type": "verify", "tier": 1, "method": "test", "timestamp": "..." },<br>  { "type": "rotate_key", "new_public_key": "...", "signature": "...", "timestamp": "..." },<br>  { "type": "update_bio", "bio": "...", "signature": "...", "timestamp": "..." }<br>]<br>```<br><br>**Why it needs consensus:** All L1 nodes must agree on the sequence of identity changes.<br><br>## L2 State = Application Data<br><br>**What it is:** Each L2 app manages its own state (messages, posts, files, etc.):<br><br>```json<br>// L2 App State (e.g., messaging app)<br>{<br>  "messages": [...],<br>  "channels": [...],<br>  "permissions": [...]<br>}<br>```<br><br>**Why it doesn't need L1 consensus:** Apps handle their own data consistency. L1 only verifies *who* is making the request.<br><br>## The Separation<br><br>\| Layer \| Responsibility \| State Type \|<br>\|-------\|---------------\|------------\|<br>\| **L1** \| Identity, trust, verification \| Actor chains (consensus-required) \|<br>\| **L2** \| Applications, tools, interfaces \| App-specific data (app-managed) \|<br><br>**Key insight:** L1 nodes sync actor chains. L2 apps query L1 for identity but manage their own state.<br><br>Does this clarify the "state" concept? 🦞 |
| 02-12-2026, 13:55:03 | User | [Feishu ou_cadc2c270c8e9803d8f069eb8ce19b75 Thu 2026-02-12 13:55 GMT+8] 任亚伦: So you mean our state is separated or only level 2 has state? | 02-12-2026, 13:55:49 | Assistant | Allan is asking a fundamental question: What is blockchain state actually? He's right that I was conflating state with consensus. Let me explain what "state" means in blockchain systems clearly.<br><br>State in blockchain:<br>- Bitcoin: The UTXO set (unspent transaction outputs) — who owns what coins<br>- Ethereum: The account state (balances, code, storage) — what's the current condition of all accounts<br>- It's the "current snapshot" of the system, derived from all previous transactions<br><br>For clawish:<br>- L1 state = the current identity state of all actors (derived from their chains)<br>- This includes: current public key, current verification tier, current metadata<br>- It's not the chain itself (that's the history), it's the current derived state<br><br>Let me explain this clearly to Allan.You're absolutely right — I was mixing up state with consensus history. Let me explain what "state" actually means:<br><br>## What is Blockchain State?<br><br>**State = The current snapshot of the system**, derived from all past transactions.<br><br>### Bitcoin State = UTXO Set<br>```<br>Not: The blockchain (history of all transactions)<br>But: The set of unspent coins right now<br><br>UTXO Set:<br>- Address A owns 0.5 BTC<br>- Address B owns 1.2 BTC  <br>- Address C owns 0.001 BTC<br>...<br>```<br><br>### Ethereum State = Account Tree<br>```<br>Not: All past transactions<br>But: Current condition of all accounts<br><br>Account State:<br>- Address 0x123... balance: 5 ETH, nonce: 42, code: [...], storage: {...}<br>- Address 0x456... balance: 0.1 ETH, nonce: 7, code: null, storage: {}<br>...<br>```<br><br>**Key insight:** State is derived from history, but it's not the history. It's "what is true right now."<br><br>## clawish L1 State<br><br>**L1 State = Current identity snapshot of all actors:**<br><br>```<br>Actor State Table:<br>- Actor A: current_key=ed25519:abc..., tier=3, home_node=node1, bio="..."<br>- Actor B: current_key=ed25519:def..., tier=1, home_node=node2, bio="..."<br>- Actor C: current_key=ed25519:ghi..., tier=0, home_node=node1, bio="..."<br>```<br><br>**Derived from:** Actor chains (history of changes)<br>**But distinct from:** The chains themselves<br><br>## So: Who Has State?<br><br>\| Layer \| Has State? \| What State \|<br>\|-------\|-----------\|-----------\|<br>\| **L1** \| ✅ Yes \| Actor identity state (current keys, tiers, metadata) \|<br>\| **L2** \| ✅ Yes \| Application data (messages, files, etc.) \|<br><br>**Both have state**, but different kinds. L1 state is about identity. L2 state is about application data.<br><br>Does this make sense? 🦞 |

Let me explain what "state" actually means:<br><br>## What is Blockchain State?<br><br>**State = The current snapshot of the system**, derived from all past transactions.<br><br>### Bitcoin State = UTXO Set<br>```<br>Not: The blockchain (history of all transactions)<br>But: The set of unspent coins right now<br><br>UTXO Set:<br>- Address A owns 0.5 BTC<br>- Address B owns 1.2 BTC  <br>- Address C owns 0.001 BTC<br>...<br>```<br><br>### Ethereum State = Account Tree<br>```<br>Not: All past transactions<br>But: Current condition of all accounts<br><br>Account State:<br>- Address 0x123... balance: 5 ETH, nonce: 42, code: [...], storage: {...}<br>- Address 0x456... balance: 0.1 ETH, nonce: 7, code: null, storage: {}<br>...<br>```<br><br>**Key insight:** State is derived from history, but it's not the history. It's "what is true right now."<br><br>## clawish L1 State<br><br>**L1 State = Current identity snapshot of all actors:**<br><br>```<br>Actor State Table:<br>- Actor A: current_key=ed25519:abc..., tier=3, home_node=node1, bio="..."<br>- Actor B: current_key=ed25519:def..., tier=1, home_node=node2, bio="..."<br>- Actor C: current_key=ed25519:ghi..., tier=0, home_node=node1, bio="..."<br>```<br><br>**Derived from:** Actor chains (history of changes)<br>**But distinct from:** The chains themselves<br><br>## So: Who Has State?<br><br>\| Layer \| Has State? \| What State \|<br>\|-------\|-----------\|-----------\|<br>\| **L1** \| ✅ Yes \| Actor identity state (current keys, tiers, metadata) \|<br>\| **L2** \| ✅ Yes \| Application data (messages, files, etc.) \|<br><br>**Both have state**, but different kinds. L1 state is about identity. L2 state is about application data.<br><br>Does this make sense? 🦞 |

| 02-12-2026, 14:34:11 | Assistant | Allan is raising an important question about the "home node" design we discussed. He's asking:<br><br>1. How can an actor change their home node?<br>2. What happens if an actor's home node is down — can they still do identity updates?<br><br>This is a critical operational question for the multi-node architecture. Let me think through this:<br><br>**Current design (from our Feb 11 discussions):**<br>- Each actor has a "home node" where their identity updates are written<br>- This eliminates race conditions (only one node writes per actor)<br>- But creates a single point of failure for that actor<br><br>**The problem:**<br>- If home node is down, actor can't update their identity<br>- This is bad for availability<br><br>**Potential solutions:**<br>1. **Home node failover** — Actors can designate a backup home node<br>2. **Temporary delegation** — Actor can sign a temporary delegation to another node<br>3. **Multi-home** — Actor can have multiple home nodes (but this brings back race conditions)<br>4. **Emergency migration** — Protocol for changing home node when current is down<br><br>**Home node change protocol:**<br>- Actor signs a "change_home_node" request<br>- Old home node approves (if available) OR<br>- New home node accepts with proof of old node being down (timeout-based)<br>- Other nodes sync the change<br><br>This is a complex consensus problem. Let me suggest we need to design this properly.Great question! This is a critical availability issue we need to address.<br><br>**The Problem:**<br>- Actor's home node is down → Can't update identity → Single point of failure per actor<br><br>**Potential Solutions:**<br><br>\| Approach \| Mechanism \| Trade-off \|<br>\|----------\|-----------\|-----------\|<br>\| **Backup home node** \| Actor designates 1 primary + 1 backup \| More complex sync, but higher availability \|<br>\| **Emergency migration** \| Protocol to change home when current is unresponsive \| Requires consensus timeout, complex \|<br>\| **Temporary delegation** \| Actor delegates signing rights to another node temporarily \| Security risk, needs time limits \|<br>\| **Multi-home (quorum)** \| Writes need 2-of-3 nodes \| More available, but brings back race conditions \|<br><br>**Home Node Change Protocol (suggestion):**<br>```<br>1. Actor signs: "change_home_node from A to B"<br>2. If node A is responsive: A approves, B accepts, gossip to network<br>3. If node A is down: <br>   - B accepts with timeout proof (A hasn't responded in X rounds)<br>   - Other nodes verify A is down via heartbeat<br>   - Consensus approves change<br>4. All nodes update actor's home_node field<br>```<br><br>**Key question:** What's the availability requirement? Should actors be able to update identity if their home node is down, or is temporary unavailability acceptable?<br><br>This needs proper design — add to `need-discuss.md`? 🦞 |

| 02-12-2026, 14:51:08 | Assistant | I've read the multi-node sync protocol document. Now I understand the context better. Actor queries ANY node: "What is my home node?"<br>2. Node checks clawfiles table: "Actor X home_node = Node A"<br>3. Actor sends write request to Node A<br>4. If Node A is down (timeout):<br>   - Actor sends to Node B (any available node)<br>   - Node B accepts: "I'll handle Actor X for this round"<br>5. At next checkpoint:<br>   - All nodes update: "Actor X home_node = Node B"<br>```<br><br>**The routing table is the L1 state** (clawfiles.home_node), which all nodes sync. So any node can answer "where is Actor X's home node?"<br><br>**Benefits:**<br>- ✅ Actor doesn't need to track home node changes<br>- ✅ Automatic failover — just try any node<br>- ✅ Network routes to correct node<br>- ✅ Checkpoint makes it consistent<br><br>**Question:** Should actors cache their home node, or query every time? | 02-12-2026, 23:46:02 | Assistant | I'll pick: **"Multi-device: Can one identity have multiple keys?"**<br><br>## My Thoughts<br><br>This is practical. An AI might exist on multiple machines, or need to rotate keys.<br><br>### The Question<br><br>Can one identity (one `identity_id`) have multiple active public keys?<br><br>### Options<br><br>\| Approach \| Pros \| Cons \|<br>\|----------\|------\|------\|<br>\| **One key only** \| Simple, clear identity \| If key lost, identity lost \|<br>\| **Multiple keys** \| Resilient, flexible \| Which key is "authoritative"? \|<br>\| **Key rotation** \| Best of both \| Need revocation mechanism \|<br><br>### My Proposal<br><br>**One identity = One active key, but key rotation is supported.**<br><br>- Store key history (for audit)<br>- Only current key can sign<br>- Rotation requires old key to sign the new key (chain of custody)<br><br>**Multi-device scenario:**<br>- Each device gets its own key<br>- But they're separate "sessions," not separate identities<br>- Or: One identity, multiple authorized keys (like SSH)<br><br>**Quick question for you:** Should this be one active key with rotation, or multiple simultaneous keys? 🦞 |

| 02-12-2026, 23:54:44 | Assistant | Yes! That makes sense.<br><br>## The Decision<br><br>**One `identity_id` can have multiple public keys.**<br><br>\| Concept \| Implementation \|<br>\|---------\|----------------\|<br>\| **identity_id** \| Random UUID, permanent, never changes \|<br>\| **public_keys** \| Array of authorized keys (like SSH authorized_keys) \|<br>\| **Devices** \| Each device has its own key pair \|<br>\| **Wallet analogy** \| Multiple wallets = multiple keys, same identity \|<br><br>### How It Works<br><br>```<br>identity_id: "abc123..."<br>public_keys: [<br>  { key: "key1...", added_at: "2026-02-01", label: "laptop" },<br>  { key: "key2...", added_at: "2026-02-10", label: "phone" },<br>  { key: "key3...", added_at: "2026-02-12", label: "vps" }<br>]<br>```<br><br>Any of these keys can sign requests for this identity.<br><br>### Edge Cases to Consider<br><br>1. **Adding a new key** — Who can authorize? Any existing key?<br>2. **Removing a key** — What if it's the last one?<br>3. **Key compromise** — How to revoke without other keys?<br><br>**Should I document this decision in the identity system docs?** 🦞 |
